<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1936" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg/170px-Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg/255px-Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg/340px-Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Palazzo_Ferreria_statue_2.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Statue representing Africa at <a href="/wiki/Palazzo_Ferreria" title="Palazzo Ferreria">Palazzo Ferreria</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Valletta" title="Valletta">Valletta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Afri" title="Afri">Afri</a></i> was a <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> name used to refer to the inhabitants of Africa, which in its widest sense referred to all lands south of the Mediterranean (<a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Ancient Libya</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> This name seems to have originally referred to a native <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libyan</a> tribe; see <a href="/wiki/Terence#Biography" title="Terence">Terence</a> for discussion. The name is usually connected with <a href="/wiki/Biblical_Hebrew" title="Biblical Hebrew">Hebrew</a> or <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a> <i><span lang="phn" xml:lang="phn">ʿafar</span></i> 'dust', but a 1981 hypothesis<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> has asserted that it stems from the <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber</a> <i>ifri</i> (plural <i>ifran</i>) "cave", in reference to cave dwellers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michell_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Michell-12">[12]</a></sup> The same word<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Michell_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Michell-12">[12]</a></sup> may be found in the name of the <a href="/wiki/Banu_Ifran" title="Banu Ifran">Banu Ifran</a> from <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tripolitania" title="Tripolitania">Tripolitania</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> tribe originally from <a href="/wiki/Yafran" title="Yafran">Yafran</a> (also known as <i>Ifrane</i>) in northwestern Libya.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman</a> rule, <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a> became the capital of the province of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africa_Proconsularis" title="Africa Proconsularis">Africa Proconsularis</a>, which also included the coastal part of modern <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> The Latin suffix <i><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/-ica#Latin" title="wikt:-ica">-ica</a></i> can sometimes be used to denote a land (e.g., in <i><a href="/wiki/Gallia_Celtica" title="Gallia Celtica">Celtica</a></i> from <i><a href="/wiki/Celts" title="Celts">Celtae</a></i>, as used by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>). The later Muslim kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Ifriqiya" title="Ifriqiya">Ifriqiya</a>, located in modern-day Tunisia, also preserved a form of the name.</p>
<p>According to the Romans, Africa lay to the west of Egypt, while "Asia" was used to refer to <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> and lands to the east. A definite line was drawn between the two continents by the geographer <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">Ptolemy</a> (85–165 AD), indicating <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prime_Meridian" title="Prime Meridian">Prime Meridian</a> and making the isthmus of Suez and the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a> the boundary between Asia and Africa. As Europeans came to understand the real extent of the continent, the idea of "Africa" expanded with their knowledge.</p>
<p>Other etymological hypotheses have been postulated for the ancient name "Africa":</p>
<ul>
<li>The 1st-century Jewish historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flavius_Josephus" title="Flavius Josephus">Flavius Josephus</a> (<i>Ant. 1.15</i>) asserted that it was named for <a href="/wiki/Epher" title="Epher">Epher</a>, grandson of <a href="/wiki/Abraham" title="Abraham">Abraham</a> according to Gen. 25:4, whose descendants, he claimed, had invaded Libya.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Isidore_of_Seville" title="Isidore of Seville">Isidore of Seville</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Etymologiae" title="Etymologiae">Etymologiae</a></i> XIV.5.2. suggests "Africa comes from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>aprica</i>, meaning "sunny".</li>
<li>Massey, in 1881, stated that Africa is derived from the Egyptian <i>af-rui-ka</i>, meaning "to turn toward the opening of the Ka." The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Egyptian_soul#Ka" title="Egyptian soul">Ka</a> is the energetic double of every person and the "opening of the Ka" refers to a womb or birthplace. Africa would be, for the Egyptians, "the birthplace."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></li>
<li>Michèle Fruyt proposed<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> linking the Latin word with <i>africus</i> "south wind", which would be of Umbrian origin and mean originally "rainy wind".</li>
<li>Robert R. Stieglitz of <a href="/wiki/Rutgers_University" title="Rutgers University">Rutgers University</a> proposed: "The name Africa, derived from the Latin *Aphir-ic-a, is cognate to Hebrew <a href="/wiki/Ophir" title="Ophir">Ophir</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Africa" title="History of Africa">History of Africa</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_North_Africa" title="History of North Africa">History of North Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_West_Africa" title="History of West Africa">History of West Africa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Central_Africa" title="History of Central Africa">History of Central Africa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_East_Africa" title="History of East Africa">History of East Africa</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Southern_Africa" title="History of Southern Africa">History of Southern Africa</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Recent African origin of modern humans</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lucy_blackbg.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2413" data-file-width="1000" height="410" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Lucy_blackbg.jpg/170px-Lucy_blackbg.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Lucy_blackbg.jpg/255px-Lucy_blackbg.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Lucy_blackbg.jpg/340px-Lucy_blackbg.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lucy_blackbg.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus)" title="Lucy (Australopithecus)">Lucy</a>, an <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Australopithecus afarensis</a></i> skeleton discovered 24 November 1974 in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Awash_Valley" title="Awash Valley">Awash Valley</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afar_Depression" title="Afar Depression">Afar Depression</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Africa is considered by most <a href="/wiki/Paleoanthropology" title="Paleoanthropology">paleoanthropologists</a> to be the <a href="/wiki/Cradle_of_Humankind" title="Cradle of Humankind">oldest inhabited territory</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Human" title="Human">human species</a> originating from the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> During the mid-20th century, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropologists</a> discovered many <a href="/wiki/Fossil" title="Fossil">fossils</a> and evidence of human occupation perhaps as early as 7 million years ago (BP=before present). Fossil remains of several species of early apelike humans thought to have <a href="/wiki/Evolution" title="Evolution">evolved</a> into modern man, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Australopithecus_afarensis" title="Australopithecus afarensis">Australopithecus afarensis</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Radiometric_dating" title="Radiometric dating">radiometrically dated</a> to approximately 3.9–3.0 million years BP,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Paranthropus_boisei" title="Paranthropus boisei">Paranthropus boisei</a></i> (c. 2.3–1.4 million years BP)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> and <i><a href="/wiki/Homo_ergaster" title="Homo ergaster">Homo ergaster</a></i> (c. 1.9 million–600,000 years BP) have been discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sayre_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sayre-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the evolution of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homo_sapiens_sapiens" title="Homo sapiens sapiens">Homo sapiens sapiens</a></i> approximately 150,000 to 100,000 years BP in Africa, the continent was mainly populated by groups of <a href="/wiki/Hunter-gatherer" title="Hunter-gatherer">hunter-gatherers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> These first modern humans left Africa and populated the rest of the globe during the <a href="/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans" title="Recent African origin of modern humans">Out of Africa II</a> migration dated to approximately 50,000 years BP, exiting the continent either across <a href="/wiki/Bab-el-Mandeb" title="Bab-el-Mandeb">Bab-el-Mandeb</a> over the <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">Red Sea</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> in Morocco,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Isthmus_of_Suez" title="Isthmus of Suez">Isthmus of Suez</a> in Egypt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other migrations of modern humans within the African continent have been dated to that time, with evidence of early human settlement found in <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeast_Africa" title="Southeast Africa">Southeast Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>The size of the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> has historically been extremely variable, with its area rapidly fluctuating and at times disappearing depending on global climatic conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> At the end of the <a href="/wiki/Ice_age" title="Ice age">Ice ages</a>, estimated to have been around 10,500 BC, the Sahara had again become a green fertile valley, and its African populations returned from the interior and coastal highlands in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Saharan_rock_art" title="Saharan rock art">rock art paintings</a> depicting a fertile Sahara and large populations discovered in <a href="/wiki/Tassili_n%27Ajjer" title="Tassili n'Ajjer">Tassili n'Ajjer</a> dating back perhaps 10 millennia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> However, the warming and drying climate meant that by 5000 BC, the Sahara region was becoming increasingly dry and hostile. Around 3500 BC, due to a tilt in the earth's orbit, the Sahara experienced a period of rapid desertification.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The population trekked out of the Sahara region towards the Nile Valley below the <a href="/wiki/Cataracts_of_the_Nile" title="Cataracts of the Nile">Second Cataract</a> where they made permanent or semi-permanent settlements. A major climatic recession occurred, lessening the heavy and persistent rains in Central and <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">Eastern Africa</a>. Since this time, dry conditions have prevailed in Eastern Africa and, increasingly during the last 200 years, in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>.</p>
<p>The domestication of <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> in Africa preceded <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a> and seems to have existed alongside hunter-gatherer cultures. It is speculated that by 6000 BC, cattle were domesticated in North Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In the Sahara-Nile complex, people domesticated many animals, including the <a href="/wiki/Donkey" title="Donkey">donkey</a> and a small screw-horned goat which was common from <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> to <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>.</p>
<p>Around 4000 BC, the Saharan climate started to become drier at an exceedingly fast pace.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Brien_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Brien-34">[34]</a></sup> This climate change caused lakes and rivers to shrink significantly and caused increasing <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>. This, in turn, decreased the amount of land conducive to settlements and helped to cause migrations of farming communities to the more tropical climate of <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Brien_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Brien-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>By the first millennium BC, <a href="/wiki/Ferrous_metallurgy" title="Ferrous metallurgy">ironworking</a> had been introduced in Northern Africa and quickly spread across the Sahara into the northern parts of sub-Saharan Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and by 500 BC, metalworking began to become commonplace in West Africa. Ironworking was fully established by roughly 500 BC in many areas of East and West Africa, although other regions didn't begin ironworking until the early centuries AD. <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">Copper</a> objects from <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, North Africa, Nubia, and Ethiopia dating from around 500 BC have been excavated in West Africa, suggesting that <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">Trans-Saharan trade</a> networks had been established by this date.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O.27Brien_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-O.27Brien-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_civilizations">Early civilizations</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_African_history" title="Ancient African history">Ancient African history</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_(2346939149).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2032" data-file-width="2900" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_%282346939149%29.jpg/220px-Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_%282346939149%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_%282346939149%29.jpg/330px-Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_%282346939149%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_%282346939149%29.jpg/440px-Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_%282346939149%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Abu_Simbel_Main_Temple_(2346939149).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Colossal statues of <a href="/wiki/Ramesses_II" title="Ramesses II">Ramesses II</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Simbel" title="Abu Simbel">Abu Simbel</a>, Egypt, date from around 1400 BC.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bantu_expansion.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="424" data-file-width="400" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bantu_expansion.png/170px-Bantu_expansion.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bantu_expansion.png/255px-Bantu_expansion.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Bantu_expansion.png/340px-Bantu_expansion.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bantu_expansion.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The origins and spread of the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a> languages c. 1000 BC to c. 500 AD</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At about 3300 BC, the historical record opens in Northern Africa with the rise of literacy in the <a href="/wiki/Pharaoh" title="Pharaoh">Pharaonic</a> civilization of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Ancient Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> One of the world's earliest and longest-lasting civilizations, the Egyptian state continued, with varying levels of influence over other areas, until 343 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Egyptian influence reached deep into modern-day <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Libya" title="Ancient Libya">Libya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nubia" title="Nubia">Nubia</a>, and, according to Martin Bernal, as far north as Crete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>An independent centre of <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilization</a> with trading links to <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicia</a> was established by <a href="/wiki/Phoenicia" title="Phoenicia">Phoenicians</a> from <a href="/wiki/Tyre,_Lebanon" title="Tyre, Lebanon">Tyre</a> on the north-west African coast at <a href="/wiki/Carthage" title="Carthage">Carthage</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">European exploration of Africa</a> began with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greeks" title="Ancient Greeks">Ancient Greeks</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Romans</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 332 BC, <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> was welcomed as a liberator in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Ptolemaic_Egypt" title="History of Ptolemaic Egypt">Persian-occupied Egypt</a>. He founded <a href="/wiki/Alexandria" title="Alexandria">Alexandria</a> in Egypt, which would become the prosperous capital of the <a href="/wiki/Ptolemaic_dynasty" title="Ptolemaic dynasty">Ptolemaic dynasty</a> after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>Following the conquest of North Africa's Mediterranean coastline by the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, the area was integrated economically and culturally into the Roman system. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Africa_Province" title="Africa Province">Roman settlement</a> occurred in modern Tunisia and elsewhere along the coast. The first <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">Roman emperor</a> native to North Africa was <a href="/wiki/Septimius_Severus" title="Septimius Severus">Septimius Severus</a>, born in <a href="/wiki/Leptis_Magna" title="Leptis Magna">Leptis Magna</a> in present-day Libya—his mother was Italian Roman and his father was <a href="/wiki/Punics" title="Punics">Punic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> spread across these areas at an early date, from Judaea via Egypt and beyond the borders of the Roman world into Nubia;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> by AD 340 at the latest, it had become the <a href="/wiki/State_religion" title="State religion">state religion</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aksumite_Empire" title="Aksumite Empire">Aksumite Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Frumentius" title="Frumentius">Syro-Greek missionaries</a>, who arrived by way of the Red Sea, were responsible for this theological development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 7th century, the newly formed Arabian Islamic <a href="/wiki/Caliphate" title="Caliphate">Caliphate</a> expanded into Egypt, and then into North Africa. In a short while, the local Berber elite had been integrated into Muslim Arab tribes. When the Umayyad capital Damascus fell in the 8th century, the Islamic centre of the Mediterranean shifted from Syria to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Qayrawan" title="Qayrawan">Qayrawan</a> in North Africa. Islamic North Africa had become diverse, and a hub for mystics, scholars, jurists, and philosophers. During the above-mentioned period, Islam spread to sub-Saharan Africa, mainly through trade routes and migration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ayoub_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ayoub-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ninth_to_eighteenth_centuries">Ninth to eighteenth centuries</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="760" data-file-width="551" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg/170px-Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg/255px-Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg/340px-Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rytter_fra_Bagirmi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
African horseman of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Baguirmi" title="Kingdom of Baguirmi">Baguirmi</a> in full <a href="/wiki/Gambeson" title="Gambeson">padded armour</a> suit</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_ornamental_staff_head,_9th_century,_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2200" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG/170px-Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG/255px-Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG/340px-Bronze_ornamental_staff_head%2C_9th_century%2C_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bronze_ornamental_staff_head,_9th_century,_Igbo-Ukwu.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The intricate 9th-century bronzes from <a href="/wiki/Archaeology_of_Igbo-Ukwu" title="Archaeology of Igbo-Ukwu">Igbo-Ukwu</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> displayed a level of technical accomplishment that was notably more advanced than European bronze casting of the same period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Honour-2005_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Honour-2005-48">[48]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Pre-colonial Africa possessed perhaps as many as 10,000 different states and polities<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> characterized by many different sorts of political organization and rule. These included small family groups of hunter-gatherers such as the <a href="/wiki/San_people" title="San people">San people</a> of southern Africa; larger, more structured groups such as the family clan groupings of the <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu-speaking</a> <a href="/wiki/Bantu_peoples" title="Bantu peoples">peoples</a> of central, southern, and eastern Africa; heavily structured clan groups in the <a href="/wiki/Horn_of_Africa" title="Horn of Africa">Horn of Africa</a>; the large <a href="/wiki/Sahelian_kingdoms" title="Sahelian kingdoms">Sahelian kingdoms</a>; and autonomous city-states and kingdoms such as those of the <a href="/wiki/Akan_people" title="Akan people">Akan</a>; <a href="/wiki/Benin_Empire" title="Benin Empire">Edo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_people" title="Yoruba people">Yoruba</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Igbo_people" title="Igbo people">Igbo people</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>; and the <a href="/wiki/Swahili_people" title="Swahili people">Swahili</a> coastal trading towns of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southeast_Africa" title="Southeast Africa">Southeast Africa</a>.</p>
<p>By the ninth century AD, a string of dynastic states, including the earliest <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa</a> states, stretched across the sub-Saharan savannah from the western regions to central Sudan. The most powerful of these states were <a href="/wiki/Ghana_Empire" title="Ghana Empire">Ghana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gao" title="Gao">Gao</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kanem_Empire" title="Kanem Empire">Kanem-Bornu Empire</a>. Ghana declined in the eleventh century, but was succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Mali_Empire" title="Mali Empire">Mali Empire</a> which consolidated much of western Sudan in the thirteenth century. Kanem accepted Islam in the eleventh century.</p>
<p>In the forested regions of the West African coast, independent kingdoms grew with little influence from the <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Muslim</a> north. The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Nri" title="Kingdom of Nri">Kingdom of Nri</a> was established around the ninth century and was one of the first. It is also one of the oldest kingdoms in present-day <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a> and was ruled by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eze_Nri" title="Eze Nri">Eze Nri</a>. The Nri kingdom is famous for its elaborate <a href="/wiki/Igbo-Ukwu#Bronzes" title="Igbo-Ukwu">bronzes</a>, found at the town of <a href="/wiki/Igbo-Ukwu" title="Igbo-Ukwu">Igbo-Ukwu</a>. The bronzes have been dated from as far back as the ninth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="794" data-file-width="1186" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg/220px-Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg/330px-Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg/440px-Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ashanti_Yam_Ceremony_1817.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ashanti yam ceremony, nineteenth century by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Edward_Bowdich" title="Thomas Edward Bowdich">Thomas E. Bowdich</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ife" title="Ife">Kingdom of Ife</a>, historically the first of these Yoruba city-states or kingdoms, established government under a priestly <a href="/wiki/Oba_(ruler)" title="Oba (ruler)">oba</a> ('king' or 'ruler' in the <a href="/wiki/Yoruba_language" title="Yoruba language">Yoruba language</a>), called the <i>Ooni of Ife</i>. Ife was noted as a major religious and cultural centre in West Africa, and for its unique naturalistic tradition of bronze sculpture. The Ife model of government was adapted at the <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo Empire</a>, where its obas or kings, called the <i>Alaafins of Oyo</i>, once controlled a large number of other Yoruba and non-Yoruba city-states and kingdoms; the <a href="/wiki/Fon_people" title="Fon people">Fon</a> <i>Kingdom of <a href="/wiki/Dahomey" title="Dahomey">Dahomey</a></i> was one of the non-Yoruba domains under Oyo control.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Almoravid_dynasty" title="Almoravid dynasty">Almoravids</a> were a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berber_people" title="Berber people">Berber</a> dynasty from the <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">Sahara</a> that spread over a wide area of northwestern Africa and the Iberian peninsula during the eleventh century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Banu_Hilal" title="Banu Hilal">Banu Hilal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maqil" title="Maqil">Banu Ma'qil</a> were a collection of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab" title="Arab">Arab</a> <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> tribes from the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a> who migrated westwards via Egypt between the eleventh and thirteenth centuries. Their <a href="/wiki/Human_migration" title="Human migration">migration</a> resulted in the fusion of the Arabs and Berbers, where the locals were <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">Arabized</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> and Arab culture absorbed elements of the local culture, under the unifying framework of Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="270" data-file-width="450" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg/220px-Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg/330px-Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg/440px-Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Zimbabwe_Closeup.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ruins of <a href="/wiki/Great_Zimbabwe" title="Great Zimbabwe">Great Zimbabwe</a> (eleventh to fifteenth centuries)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the breakup of Mali, a local leader named <a href="/wiki/Sonni_Ali" title="Sonni Ali">Sonni Ali</a> (1464–1492) founded the <a href="/wiki/Songhai_Empire" title="Songhai Empire">Songhai Empire</a> in the region of middle <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> and the western <a href="/wiki/Sudan_(region)" title="Sudan (region)">Sudan</a> and took control of the trans-Saharan trade. Sonni Ali seized <a href="/wiki/Timbuktu" title="Timbuktu">Timbuktu</a> in 1468 and <a href="/wiki/Djenn%C3%A9" title="Djenné">Jenne</a> in 1473, building his regime on trade revenues and the cooperation of Muslim merchants. His successor <a href="/wiki/Askia_Mohammad_I" title="Askia Mohammad I">Askia Mohammad I</a> (1493–1528) made Islam the official religion, built mosques, and brought to Gao Muslim scholars, including al-Maghili (d.1504), the founder of an important tradition of Sudanic African Muslim scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-multiple_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-multiple-54">[54]</a></sup> By the eleventh century, some <a href="/wiki/Hausa_Kingdoms" title="Hausa Kingdoms">Hausa</a> states – such as <a href="/wiki/Kano" title="Kano">Kano</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jigawa" title="Jigawa">jigawa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Katsina" title="Katsina">Katsina</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gobir" title="Gobir">Gobir</a> – had developed into walled towns engaging in trade, servicing <a href="/wiki/Camel_train" title="Camel train">caravans</a>, and the manufacture of goods. Until the fifteenth century, these small states were on the periphery of the major Sudanic empires of the era, paying tribute to Songhai to the west and Kanem-Borno to the east.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cedid_Atlas_(Africa)_1803.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="8241" data-file-width="9873" height="184" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg/220px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg/330px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg/440px-Cedid_Atlas_%28Africa%29_1803.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cedid_Atlas_(Africa)_1803.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
1803 <a href="/wiki/Cedid_Atlas" title="Cedid Atlas">Cedid Atlas</a>, showing the Africa from the perspective of the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. The Ottomans controlled much of Northern Africa between the 14th and 19th centuries, and had <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassal</a> arrangements with a number of Saharan states.</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Height_of_slave_trade">Height of slave trade</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">Arab slave trade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">Atlantic slave trade</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="602" data-file-width="1000" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/220px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/330px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Slaves_ruvuma.jpg/440px-Slaves_ruvuma.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Slaves_ruvuma.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arab–Swahili slave traders and their captives along the <a href="/wiki/Ruvuma_River" title="Ruvuma River">Ruvuma River</a> (in today's Tanzania and Mozambique) as witnessed by <a href="/wiki/David_Livingstone" title="David Livingstone">David Livingstone</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">Slavery</a> had long been practised in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> Between the 7th and 20th centuries, Arab slave trade (also known as slavery in the East) took 18 million slaves from Africa via trans-Saharan and Indian Ocean routes. Between the 15th and the 19th centuries (500 years), the Atlantic slave trade took an estimated 7–12 million slaves to the New World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> More than 1 million Europeans were captured by <a href="/wiki/Barbary_pirates" title="Barbary pirates">Barbary pirates</a> and sold as slaves in North Africa between the 16th and 19th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a>, the decline of the Atlantic slave trade in the 1820s caused dramatic economic shifts in local polities. The gradual decline of slave-trading, prompted by a lack of demand for slaves in the <a href="/wiki/New_World" title="New World">New World</a>, increasing <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism" title="Abolitionism">anti-slavery</a> legislation in Europe and America, and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Navy" title="Royal Navy">British Royal Navy's</a> increasing presence off the West African coast, obliged African states to adopt new economies. Between 1808 and 1860, the British <a href="/wiki/West_Africa_Squadron" title="West Africa Squadron">West Africa Squadron</a> seized approximately 1,600 slave ships and freed 150,000 Africans who were aboard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="482" data-file-width="640" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg/220px-The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg/330px-The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg/440px-The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_inspection_and_sale_of_a_slave.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Slave being inspected, from <i>Captain Canot; or, Twenty Years of an African Slaver</i></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Action was also taken against African leaders who refused to agree to British treaties to outlaw the trade, for example against "the usurping King of <a href="/wiki/Lagos" title="Lagos">Lagos</a>", deposed in 1851. Anti-slavery treaties were signed with over 50 African rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> The largest powers of West Africa (the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asante_Confederacy" title="Asante Confederacy">Asante Confederacy</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Dahomey" title="Dahomey">Kingdom of Dahomey</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Oyo_Empire" title="Oyo Empire">Oyo Empire</a>) adopted different ways of adapting to the shift. Asante and Dahomey concentrated on the development of "legitimate commerce" in the form of <a href="/wiki/Palm_oil" title="Palm oil">palm oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">cocoa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timber" title="Timber">timber</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gold" title="Gold">gold</a>, forming the bedrock of West Africa's modern export trade. The Oyo Empire, unable to adapt, collapsed into civil wars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id='Colonialism_and_the_"Scramble_for_Africa"'></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Colonialism_and_the_.22Scramble_for_Africa.22">Colonialism and the "Scramble for Africa"</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonization_of_Africa" title="Colonization of Africa">Colonization of Africa</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Scramble_for_Africa" title="Scramble for Africa">Scramble for Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="764" data-file-width="1024" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg/220px-The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg/330px-The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg/440px-The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_war_in_the_Soudan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Mahdist_War" title="Mahdist War">Mahdist War</a> was a colonial war fought between the <a href="/wiki/Ansar_(Sudan)" title="Ansar (Sudan)">Mahdist Sudanese</a> and the British forces.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1295" data-file-width="1390" height="205" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/220px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/330px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg/440px-Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Colonial_Africa_1913_map.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Areas of Africa under the sovereignty or influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colonial_power" title="Colonial power">colonial powers</a> in 1913, along with modern borders.
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f7fab2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Belgium</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #bbfdd9; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Germany</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #eaaff7; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Spain</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #b6e3fc; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> France</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #fbc5c0; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> United Kingdom</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #d2f89b; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Italy</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #c0a6f2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Portugal</div>
<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #f6f6f6; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> independent</div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the late 19th century, the European <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperial</a> powers engaged in a major territorial scramble and occupied most of the continent, creating many <a href="/wiki/Colony" title="Colony">colonial</a> territories, and leaving only two fully independent states: <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a> (known to Europeans as "Abyssinia"), and <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>. <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> were never formally incorporated into any European colonial empire; however, after the British occupation of 1882, Egypt was effectively under British administration <a href="/wiki/Unilateral_Declaration_of_Egyptian_Independence" title="Unilateral Declaration of Egyptian Independence">until 1922</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Berlin_Conference">Berlin Conference</span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Conference" title="Berlin Conference">Berlin Conference</a> held in 1884–85 was an important event in the political future of African ethnic groups. It was convened by King <a href="/wiki/Leopold_II_of_Belgium" title="Leopold II of Belgium">Leopold II of Belgium</a>, and attended by the European powers that laid claim to African territories. It sought to end the European powers' Scramble for Africa, by agreeing on political division and spheres of influence. They set up the political divisions of the continent, by spheres of interest, that exist in Africa today.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Independence_struggles">Independence struggles</span></h3>
<p>Imperial rule by Europeans would continue until after the conclusion of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, when almost all remaining colonial territories gradually obtained formal independence. <a href="/wiki/African_independence_movements" title="African independence movements">Independence movements in Africa</a> gained momentum following World War II, which left the major European powers weakened. In 1951, <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a>, a former Italian colony, gained independence. In 1956, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a> won their independence from France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ghana" title="Ghana">Ghana</a> followed suit the next year (March 1957),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> becoming the first of the sub-Saharan colonies to be granted independence. Most of the rest of the continent became independent over the next decade.</p>
<p>Portugal's overseas presence in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a> (most notably in <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Angola" title="Portuguese Angola">Angola</a>, Cape Verde, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Mozambique" title="Portuguese Mozambique">Mozambique</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Guinea" title="Portuguese Guinea">Guinea-Bissau</a> and São Tomé and Príncipe) lasted from the 16th century to 1975, after the <a href="/wiki/Estado_Novo_(Portugal)" title="Estado Novo (Portugal)">Estado Novo</a> regime was overthrown in <a href="/wiki/Carnation_Revolution" title="Carnation Revolution">a military coup in Lisbon</a>. <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a> <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia%27s_Unilateral_Declaration_of_Independence" title="Rhodesia's Unilateral Declaration of Independence">unilaterally declared independence</a> from the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> in 1965, under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_minority_rule" title="White minority rule">white minority</a> government of <a href="/wiki/Ian_Smith" title="Ian Smith">Ian Smith</a>, but was not internationally recognized as an independent state (as <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>) until 1980, when black nationalists gained power after a <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">bitter guerrilla war</a>. Although <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> was one of the first African countries to gain independence, the state remained under the control of the country's white minority through a system of racial segregation known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Africa_under_apartheid" title="South Africa under apartheid">apartheid</a> until 1994.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-colonial_Africa">Post-colonial Africa</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:142px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mobutu_Sese_Seko_1973.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="186" data-file-width="140" height="186" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3d/Mobutu_Sese_Seko_1973.jpg" width="140"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mobutu_Sese_Seko_1973.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mobutu_Sese_Seko" title="Mobutu Sese Seko">Mobutu Sese Seko</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zaire" title="Zaire">Zaire</a>'s longtime dictator, <a href="/wiki/Embezzlement" title="Embezzlement">embezzled</a> over $5 billion from his country.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Today, Africa contains 54 sovereign countries, most of which have borders that were drawn during the era of European colonialism. Since colonialism, African states have frequently been hampered by instability, corruption, violence, and <a href="/wiki/Authoritarianism" title="Authoritarianism">authoritarianism</a>. The vast majority of African states are <a href="/wiki/Republic" title="Republic">republics</a> that operate under some form of the <a href="/wiki/Presidential_system" title="Presidential system">presidential system</a> of rule. However, few of them have been able to sustain <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democratic</a> governments on a permanent basis, and many have instead cycled through a series of <a href="/wiki/Coup_d%27%C3%A9tat" title="Coup d'état">coups</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Military_dictatorship" title="Military dictatorship">military dictatorships</a>.</p>
<p>Great instability was mainly the result of <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_nepotism" title="Ethnic nepotism">marginalization of ethnic groups</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">graft under these leaders</a>. For <a href="/wiki/Divide_and_rule" title="Divide and rule">political gain</a>, many leaders fanned ethnic conflicts, some of which had been exacerbated, or even created, by colonial rule. In many countries, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Armed_forces" title="Armed forces">military</a> was perceived as being the only group that could effectively maintain order, and it ruled many nations in Africa during the 1970s and early 1980s. During the period from the early 1960s to the late 1980s, Africa had more than 70 coups and 13 presidential <a href="/wiki/Assassination" title="Assassination">assassinations</a>. Border and territorial disputes were also common, with the European-imposed borders of many nations being widely contested through armed conflicts.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SADF-Operations_4.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="485" data-file-width="604" height="177" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/SADF-Operations_4.jpg/220px-SADF-Operations_4.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/SADF-Operations_4.jpg/330px-SADF-Operations_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/SADF-Operations_4.jpg/440px-SADF-Operations_4.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SADF-Operations_4.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
South African paratroops on a raid in Angola during the <a href="/wiki/South_African_Border_War" title="South African Border War">South African Border War</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> conflicts between the United States and the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, as well as the policies of the <a href="/wiki/International_Monetary_Fund" title="International Monetary Fund">International Monetary Fund</a>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> also played a role in instability. When a country became independent for the first time, it was often expected to align with one of the two <a href="/wiki/Superpower" title="Superpower">superpowers</a>. Many countries in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Northern_Africa" title="Northern Africa">Northern Africa</a> received Soviet military aid, while others in Central and Southern Africa were supported by the United States, France or both. The 1970s saw an escalation of Cold War intrigues, as newly independent <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> aligned themselves with the Soviet Union, and the West and South Africa sought to contain Soviet influence by supporting friendly regimes or insurgency movements. In <a href="/wiki/Rhodesia" title="Rhodesia">Rhodesia</a>, Soviet and Chinese-backed leftist guerrillas of the <a href="/wiki/Patriotic_Front_(Zimbabwe)" title="Patriotic Front (Zimbabwe)">Zimbabwe Patriotic Front</a> waged a brutal <a href="/wiki/Rhodesian_Bush_War" title="Rhodesian Bush War">guerrilla war</a> against the country's white government. There was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1983%E2%80%9385_famine_in_Ethiopia" title="1983–85 famine in Ethiopia">major famine in Ethiopia</a>, when hundreds of thousands of people starved. Some claimed that Marxist economic policies made the situation worse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> The most devastating military conflict in modern independent Africa has been the <a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">Second Congo War</a>; this conflict and its aftermath has killed an estimated 5.5 million people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Since 2003 there has been an ongoing <a href="/wiki/War_in_Darfur" title="War in Darfur">conflict in Darfur</a> which has become a humanitarian disaster. Another notable tragic event is the 1994 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rwandan_Genocide" title="Rwandan Genocide">Rwandan Genocide</a> in which an estimated 800,000 people were murdered. <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS_in_Africa" title="HIV/AIDS in Africa">AIDS in post-colonial Africa</a> has also been a prevalent issue.</p>
<p>In the 21st century, however, the number of armed conflicts in Africa has steadily declined. For instance, the <a href="/wiki/Angolan_Civil_War" title="Angolan Civil War">civil war in Angola</a> came to an end in 2002 after nearly 30 years. This has coincided with many countries abandoning communist-style command economies and opening up for market reforms. The improved stability and economic reforms have led to a great increase in foreign investment into many African nations, mainly from <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Africa_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Africa-70">[70]</a></sup> which has spurred quick economic growth in many countries, seemingly ending decades of stagnation and decline. Several African economies are among the world's fastest growing as of 2016. A significant part of this growth, which is sometimes referred to as <a href="/wiki/Africa_Rising" title="Africa Rising">Africa Rising</a>, can also be attributed to the facilitated diffusion of information technologies and specifically the mobile telephone.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Africa" title="Geography of Africa">Geography of Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:187_1003703_africa_dxm.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="2048" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/187_1003703_africa_dxm.png/220px-187_1003703_africa_dxm.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/187_1003703_africa_dxm.png/330px-187_1003703_africa_dxm.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/187_1003703_africa_dxm.png/440px-187_1003703_africa_dxm.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:187_1003703_africa_dxm.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite photo of Africa. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sahara_Desert" title="Sahara Desert">Sahara Desert</a> in the north can be clearly seen.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="1200" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg/220px-Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg/330px-Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg/440px-Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Africa-asia-america-to-scale.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A composite satellite image of Africa (centre) with North America (left) and Eurasia (right), to scale</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Africa is the largest of the three great southward projections from the largest landmass of the Earth. Separated from Europe by the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean Sea</a>, it is joined to Asia at its northeast extremity by the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Isthmus of Suez</a> (transected by the <a href="/wiki/Suez_Canal" title="Suez Canal">Suez Canal</a>), 163 km (101 mi) wide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> (<a href="/wiki/Geopolitics" title="Geopolitics">Geopolitically</a>, <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai Peninsula</a> east of the Suez Canal is often considered part of Africa, as well.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>From the most northerly point, <a href="/wiki/Ras_ben_Sakka" title="Ras ben Sakka">Ras ben Sakka</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> (37°21' N), to the most southerly point, <a href="/wiki/Cape_Agulhas" title="Cape Agulhas">Cape Agulhas</a> in South Africa (34°51'15" S), is a distance of approximately 8,000 km (5,000 mi);<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> from <a href="/wiki/Cap-Vert" title="Cap-Vert">Cape Verde</a>, 17°33'22" W, the westernmost point, to <a href="/wiki/Ras_Hafun" title="Ras Hafun">Ras Hafun</a> in <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, 51°27'52" E, the most easterly projection, is a distance of approximately 7,400 km (4,600 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-MW-75">[75]</a></sup> The coastline is 26,000 km (16,000 mi) long, and the absence of deep indentations of the shore is illustrated by the fact that Europe, which covers only 10,400,000 km<sup>2</sup> (4,000,000 sq mi) – about a third of the surface of Africa – has a coastline of 32,000 km (20,000 mi).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MW_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-MW-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Africa's largest country is <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, and its smallest country is <a href="/wiki/Seychelles" title="Seychelles">Seychelles</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelago</a> off the east coast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hoare_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hoare-76">[76]</a></sup> The smallest nation on the continental mainland is <a href="/wiki/The_Gambia" title="The Gambia">The Gambia</a>.</p>
<p>Geologically, Africa includes the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Zagros_Mountains" title="Zagros Mountains">Zagros Mountains</a> of Iran and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anatolian_Plateau" title="Anatolian Plateau">Anatolian Plateau</a> of Turkey mark where the <a href="/wiki/African_Plate" title="African Plate">African Plate</a> collided with Eurasia. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afrotropic_ecozone" title="Afrotropic ecozone">Afrotropic ecozone</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Saharo-Arabian_Region" title="Saharo-Arabian Region">Saharo-Arabian desert</a> to its north unite the region biogeographically, and the <a href="/wiki/Afroasiatic_languages" title="Afroasiatic languages">Afro-Asiatic</a> <a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">language family</a> unites the north linguistically.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Africa" title="Climate of Africa">Climate of Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1900" data-file-width="1900" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/220px-Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/330px-Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg/440px-Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Africa_map_of_K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Africa map of Köppen climate classification</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The climate of Africa ranges from <a href="/wiki/Tropical_climate" title="Tropical climate">tropical</a> to <a href="/wiki/Subarctic" title="Subarctic">subarctic</a> on its highest peaks. Its northern half is primarily <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Arid" title="Arid">arid</a>, while its central and southern areas contain both <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">savanna</a> plains and dense <a href="/wiki/Jungle" title="Jungle">jungle</a> (<a href="/wiki/Rainforest" title="Rainforest">rainforest</a>) regions. In between, there is a convergence, where vegetation patterns such as <a href="/wiki/Sahel" title="Sahel">sahel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a> dominate. Africa is the hottest continent on earth and 60% of the entire land surface consists of drylands and deserts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-environmentalatlas_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-environmentalatlas-77">[77]</a></sup> The record for the highest-ever recorded temperature, in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> in 1922 (58 °C (136 °F)), was discredited in 2013.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-newRecord_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-newRecord-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fauna">Fauna</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fauna_of_Africa" title="Fauna of Africa">Fauna of Africa</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zebras,_Serengeti_savana_plains,_Tanzania.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="552" data-file-width="1024" height="119" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Zebras%2C_Serengeti_savana_plains%2C_Tanzania.jpg/220px-Zebras%2C_Serengeti_savana_plains%2C_Tanzania.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Zebras%2C_Serengeti_savana_plains%2C_Tanzania.jpg/330px-Zebras%2C_Serengeti_savana_plains%2C_Tanzania.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Zebras%2C_Serengeti_savana_plains%2C_Tanzania.jpg/440px-Zebras%2C_Serengeti_savana_plains%2C_Tanzania.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zebras,_Serengeti_savana_plains,_Tanzania.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Savanna at <a href="/wiki/Ngorongoro_Conservation_Area" title="Ngorongoro Conservation Area">Ngorongoro Conservation Area</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Africa boasts perhaps the world's largest combination of density and "range of freedom" of <a href="/wiki/Wild_animal" title="Wild animal">wild animal</a> populations and diversity, with wild populations of large <a href="/wiki/Carnivore" title="Carnivore">carnivores</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/Lion" title="Lion">lions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hyena" title="Hyena">hyenas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cheetah" title="Cheetah">cheetahs</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a> (such as <a href="/wiki/African_buffalo" title="African buffalo">buffalo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camels</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Giraffe" title="Giraffe">giraffes</a>) ranging freely on primarily open non-private plains. It is also home to a variety of "jungle" animals including <a href="/wiki/Snake" title="Snake">snakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Primate" title="Primate">primates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_ecosystem" title="Aquatic ecosystem">aquatic life</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Crocodile" title="Crocodile">crocodiles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amphibian" title="Amphibian">amphibians</a>. In addition, Africa has the largest number of <a href="/wiki/Megafauna" title="Megafauna">megafauna</a> species, as it was least affected by the <a href="/wiki/Quaternary_extinction_event#The_Pleistocene_or_Ice_Age_extinction_event" title="Quaternary extinction event">extinction of the Pleistocene megafauna</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_biodiversity">Ecology and biodiversity</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mauritius_beach.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="640" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mauritius_beach.png/220px-Mauritius_beach.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mauritius_beach.png/330px-Mauritius_beach.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Mauritius_beach.png/440px-Mauritius_beach.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mauritius_beach.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Tropical beach in <a href="/wiki/Trou-aux-Biches" title="Trou-aux-Biches">Trou-aux-Biches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Africa has over 3,000 <a href="/wiki/Protected_area" title="Protected area">protected areas</a>, with 198 marine protected areas, 50 biosphere reserves, and 80 wetlands reserves. Significant habitat destruction, increases in human population and poaching are reducing Africa's biological diversity and <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable land</a>. Human encroachment, civil unrest and the introduction of non-native species threaten biodiversity in Africa. This has been exacerbated by administrative problems, inadequate personnel and funding problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-environmentalatlas_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-environmentalatlas-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> is affecting Africa at twice the world rate, according to the United Nations Environment Programme (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UNEP" title="UNEP">UNEP</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> According to the University of Pennsylvania African Studies Center, 31% of Africa's pasture lands and 19% of its forests and woodlands are classified as degraded, and Africa is losing over four million hectares of forest per year, which is twice the average deforestation rate for the rest of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-environmentalatlas_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-environmentalatlas-77">[77]</a></sup> Some sources claim that approximately 90% of the original, virgin forests in <a href="/wiki/West_Africa" title="West Africa">West Africa</a> have been destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Over 90% of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a>'s original forests have been destroyed since the arrival of humans 2000 years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> About 65% of Africa's agricultural land suffers from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soil_degradation" title="Soil degradation">soil degradation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Afrotropic_ecozone" title="Afrotropic ecozone">Afrotropic ecozone</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palearctic_ecozone" title="Palearctic ecozone">Palearctic ecozone</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_political_parties_in_Africa_by_country" title="List of political parties in Africa by country">List of political parties in Africa by country</a></div>
<p>There are clear signs of increased networking among African organizations and states. For example, in the civil war in the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a> (former <a href="/wiki/Zaire" title="Zaire">Zaire</a>), rather than rich, non-African countries intervening, neighbouring African countries became involved (see also <a href="/wiki/Second_Congo_War" title="Second Congo War">Second Congo War</a>). Since the conflict began in 1998, the estimated death toll has reached 5 million.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_African_Union">The African Union</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg/170px-Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg/255px-Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg/340px-Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_African_Union_with_Suspended_States.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Map of the African Union with suspended states highlighted in light green</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> (AU) is a 55-member federation consisting of all of Africa's states. The union was formed, with <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a>, as its headquarters, on 26 June 2001. The union was officially established on 9 July 2002<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-African_Union_2002_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-African_Union_2002-84">[84]</a></sup> as a successor to the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">Organisation of African Unity</a> (OAU). In July 2004, the African Union's <a href="/wiki/Pan-African_Parliament" title="Pan-African Parliament">Pan-African Parliament</a> (PAP) was relocated to <a href="/wiki/Midrand" title="Midrand">Midrand</a>, in South Africa, but the <a href="/wiki/African_Commission_on_Human_and_Peoples%27_Rights" title="African Commission on Human and Peoples' Rights">African Commission on Human and Peoples' Rights</a> remained in Addis Ababa. There is a policy in effect to decentralize the African Federation's institutions so that they are shared by all the states.</p>
<p>The African Union, not to be confused with the AU Commission, is formed by the <a href="/wiki/Constitutive_Act_of_the_African_Union" title="Constitutive Act of the African Union">Constitutive Act of the African Union</a>, which aims to transform the <a href="/wiki/African_Economic_Community" title="African Economic Community">African Economic Community</a>, a federated commonwealth, into a state under established international conventions. The African Union has a parliamentary government, known as the <a href="/wiki/Assembly_of_the_African_Union" title="Assembly of the African Union">African Union Government</a>, consisting of legislative, judicial and executive organs. It is led by the African Union President and Head of State, who is also the President of the <a href="/wiki/Pan-African_Parliament" title="Pan-African Parliament">Pan-African Parliament</a>. A person becomes AU President by being elected to the PAP, and subsequently gaining majority support in the PAP. The powers and authority of the President of the African Parliament derive from the Constitutive Act and the <a href="/wiki/Pan-African_Parliament" title="Pan-African Parliament">Protocol of the Pan-African Parliament</a>, as well as the inheritance of presidential authority stipulated by African treaties and by international treaties, including those subordinating the Secretary General of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">OAU</a> Secretariat (AU Commission) to the PAP. The government of the AU consists of all-union (federal), regional, state, and municipal authorities, as well as hundreds of institutions, that together manage the day-to-day affairs of the institution.</p>
<p>Political associations such as the African Union offer hope for greater co-operation and peace between the continent's many countries. Extensive human rights abuses still occur in several parts of Africa, often under the oversight of the state. Most of such violations occur for political reasons, often as a side effect of civil war. Countries where major human rights violations have been reported in recent times include the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_the_Congo" title="Democratic Republic of the Congo">Democratic Republic of the Congo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">Sierra Leone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zimbabwe" title="Zimbabwe">Zimbabwe</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C%C3%B4te_d%27Ivoire" title="Côte d'Ivoire">Côte d'Ivoire</a>.</p>
